        <title>Genome sequencing of the staple food crop white Guinea yam enables the development of a molecular marker for sex determination | BMC Biology | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Root and tuber crops are a major food source in tropical Africa. Among these crops are several species in the monocotyledonous genus Dioscorea collectively known as yam, a staple tuber crop that contributes enormously to the subsistence and socio-cultural lives of millions of people, principally in West and Central Africa. Yam cultivation is constrained by several factors, and yam can be considered a neglected &#8220;orphan&#8221; crop that would benefit from crop improvement efforts. However, the lack of genetic and genomic tools has impeded the improvement of this staple crop. To accelerate marker-assisted breeding of yam, we performed genome analysis of white Guinea yam (Dioscorea rotundata) and assembled a 594-Mb genome, 76.4% of which was distributed among 21 linkage groups. In total, we predicted 26,198 genes. Phylogenetic analyses with 2381 conserved genes revealed that Dioscorea is a unique lineage of monocotyledons distinct from the Poales (rice), Arecales (palm), and Zingiberales (banana). The entire Dioscorea genus is characterized by the occurrence of separate male and female plants (dioecy), a feature that has limited efficient yam breeding. To infer the genetics of sex determination, we performed whole-genome resequencing of bulked segregants (quantitative trait locus sequencing [QTL-seq]) in F1 progeny segregating for male and female plants and identified a genomic region associated with female heterogametic (male&#8201;=&#8201;ZZ, female&#8201;=&#8201;ZW) sex determination. We further delineated the W locus and used it to develop a molecular marker for sex identification of Guinea yam plants at the seedling stage. Guinea yam belongs to a unique and highly differentiated clade of monocotyledons. The genome analyses and sex-linked marker development performed in this study should greatly accelerate marker-assisted breeding of Guinea yam. In addition, our QTL-seq approach can be utilized in genetic studies of other outcrossing crops and organisms with highly heterozygous genomes. Genomic analysis of orphan crops such as yam promotes efforts to improve food security and the sustainability of tropical agriculture."/>

        <meta property="og:url" content="https://bmcbiol.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x"/>
        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="BMC Biology"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="Genome sequencing of the staple food crop white Guinea yam enables the development of a molecular marker for sex determination"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Root and tuber crops are a major food source in tropical Africa. Among these crops are several species in the monocotyledonous genus Dioscorea collectively known as yam, a staple tuber crop that contributes enormously to the subsistence and socio-cultural lives of millions of people, principally in West and Central Africa. Yam cultivation is constrained by several factors, and yam can be considered a neglected “orphan” crop that would benefit from crop improvement efforts. However, the lack of genetic and genomic tools has impeded the improvement of this staple crop. To accelerate marker-assisted breeding of yam, we performed genome analysis of white Guinea yam (Dioscorea rotundata) and assembled a 594-Mb genome, 76.4% of which was distributed among 21 linkage groups. In total, we predicted 26,198 genes. Phylogenetic analyses with 2381 conserved genes revealed that Dioscorea is a unique lineage of monocotyledons distinct from the Poales (rice), Arecales (palm), and Zingiberales (banana). The entire Dioscorea genus is characterized by the occurrence of separate male and female plants (dioecy), a feature that has limited efficient yam breeding. To infer the genetics of sex determination, we performed whole-genome resequencing of bulked segregants (quantitative trait locus sequencing [QTL-seq]) in F1 progeny segregating for male and female plants and identified a genomic region associated with female heterogametic (male &#x3D; ZZ, female &#x3D; ZW) sex determination. We further delineated the W locus and used it to develop a molecular marker for sex identification of Guinea yam plants at the seedling stage. Guinea yam belongs to a unique and highly differentiated clade of monocotyledons. The genome analyses and sex-linked marker development performed in this study should greatly accelerate marker-assisted breeding of Guinea yam. In addition, our QTL-seq approach can be utilized in genetic studies of other outcrossing crops and organisms with highly heterozygous genomes. Genomic analysis of orphan crops such as yam promotes efforts to improve food security and the sustainability of tropical agriculture."/>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>Root and tuber crops are a major food source in tropical Africa. Among these crops are several species in the monocotyledonous genus <i>Dioscorea</i> collectively known as yam, a staple tuber crop that contributes enormously to the subsistence and socio-cultural lives of millions of people, principally in West and Central Africa. Yam cultivation is constrained by several factors, and yam can be considered a neglected “orphan” crop that would benefit from crop improvement efforts. However, the lack of genetic and genomic tools has impeded the improvement of this staple crop.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>To accelerate marker-assisted breeding of yam, we performed genome analysis of white Guinea yam (<i>Dioscorea rotundata</i>) and assembled a 594-Mb genome, 76.4% of which was distributed among 21 linkage groups. In total, we predicted 26,198 genes. Phylogenetic analyses with 2381 conserved genes revealed that <i>Dioscorea</i> is a unique lineage of monocotyledons distinct from the Poales (rice), Arecales (palm), and Zingiberales (banana). The entire <i>Dioscorea</i> genus is characterized by the occurrence of separate male and female plants (dioecy), a feature that has limited efficient yam breeding. To infer the genetics of sex determination, we performed whole-genome resequencing of bulked segregants (quantitative trait locus sequencing [QTL-seq]) in F1 progeny segregating for male and female plants and identified a genomic region associated with female heterogametic (male = ZZ, female = ZW) sex determination. We further delineated the W locus and used it to develop a molecular marker for sex identification of Guinea yam plants at the seedling stage.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>Guinea yam belongs to a unique and highly differentiated clade of monocotyledons. The genome analyses and sex-linked marker development performed in this study should greatly accelerate marker-assisted breeding of Guinea yam. In addition, our QTL-seq approach can be utilized in genetic studies of other outcrossing crops and organisms with highly heterozygous genomes. Genomic analysis of orphan crops such as yam promotes efforts to improve food security and the sustainability of tropical agriculture.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Yam is a collective name for tuber-bearing crops belonging to the monocotyledonous <i>Dioscorea</i> genus in the family Dioscoreaceae of the order Dioscoreales. This genus contains approximately 450 species which are primarily distributed in tropical and subtropical regions worldwide [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Wilkin P, Scholsb P, Chasea MW, Chayamaritc K, Furnessa CA, Huysmansb S, Rakotonasolod F, et al. A plastid gene phylogeny of the yam genus, Dioscorea: roots, fruits and Madagascar. Syst Bot. 2005;30:736–49." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d980e996">1</a>]. Among the Dioscoreaceae, three minor genera are monoecious (having male and female flowers on a plant), but the entire genus <i>Dioscorea</i> is characterized by dioecy (the presence of separate male and female plants), a feature shared by only 5–6% of angiosperms [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Renner SS. The relative and absolute frequencies of angiosperm sexual systems: dioecy, monoecy, gynodioecy, and an updated online database. Am J Bot. 2014;101:1588–96." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d980e1002">2</a>]. The origin of <i>Dioscorea</i> is supposed to be in the Late Cretaceous (~80 Mya [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Maurin O, Muasya M, Catalan P, Shongwe EZ, Viruel J, Wilkin P, van der Bank M. Diversification into novel habitats in the Africa clade of Dioscorea (Dioscoreaceae): erect habit and elephant’s foot tubers. BMC Evol Biol. 2016;16:238." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d980e1009">3</a>]), suggesting that the origin of dioecy dates back to this time. Approximately 10 <i>Dioscorea</i> species have been independently domesticated in West Africa, Southeast Asia, and the Pacific and Caribbean islands [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lebot V. Tropical root and tuber crops: cassava, sweet potato, yams and aroids (Crop Production Science in Horticulture Series 17). Wallingford: CABI Publishing; 2009. p. 405." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d980e1015">4</a>]. <i>D. rotundata</i> is the most popular species in West and Central Africa, the main region for yam production worldwide, which contributed approximately 96% of the 63 million tons of yam produced globally in 2013 (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S1 and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S1). <i>D. rotundata</i> (white Guinea yam) and <i>D. cayenensis</i> (yellow Guinea yam) represent a major source of food and income in this region, as well as an integral part of the socio-cultural life. This geographical region is often referred to as the “civilization of the yam,” reflecting the West African societies that are tightly linked to yam cultivation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Coursey DG. The civilizations of the yam: interrelationships of man and yams in Africa and the Indo-Pacific region. Archeol Phys Anthropol Oceania. 1972;7:215–33." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d980e1034">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ayensu ES, Coursey DG. Guinea yams: the botany, ethnobotany, use and possible future of yams in West Africa. Econ Bot. 1972;26:301–18." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d980e1037">6</a>].</p><p>Despite its considerable regional importance, Guinea yam has long been regarded as an “orphan” crop, as it is not traded around the world, and it has attracted little attention from researchers and little investment. Guinea yam cultivation is constrained by several factors. Seeds are seldom used as starting materials; instead, yams are commonly propagated clonally using small whole tubers (referred to as “seed yams”) or tuber pieces. Yam is an annual climber that requires stakes for support and is highly vulnerable to a plethora of pests and diseases. Therefore, an understanding of yam genetics and a systematic improvement of yam based on crossbreeding for traits associated with tuber yield and quality, a reduced requirement for staking, and resistance/tolerance to disease and nematodes are urgently needed. Genetic analysis of <i>Dioscorea</i> has been constrained by the small number of available genetic markers. Furthermore, <i>Dioscorea</i> cultivars are highly heterozygous due to their obligate outcrossing. This heterozygosity renders genetic analysis approaches commonly used in inbreeding species, e.g., linkage analysis using the segregating progeny of an F2 generation and recombinant inbred lines (RILs), inapplicable to yam.</p><p>The International Institute of Tropical Agriculture (IITA) has a global mandate for yam research and development within the CGIAR Consortium [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="International Institute of Tropical Agriculture (IITA). &#xA;                    http://www.iita.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 1 Aug 2017." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d980e1052">7</a>]. We initiated a yam genomics program several years ago as part of an IITA-coordinated international collaboration. To generate genetic and genomic tools for yam breeding, we sequenced and assembled a highly heterozygous diploid genome of <i>D. rotundata</i>. We used this genome sequence and genetic resources to identify a locus associated with sex determination, which we used to develop a diagnostic marker for sex identification at the seedling stage. These genomic resources broaden our knowledge of Guinea yam genetics and provide a platform for implementing genomics-assisted breeding by marker-assisted selection (MAS) in this important staple crop.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Whole-genome sequencing (WGS) and assembly</h3><p>To generate a <i>D. rotundata</i> genome sequence, an individual plant, TDr96_F1, was selected from the progeny in the open-pollinated <i>D. rotundata</i> breeding line TDr96/00629 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig1">1a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig1">b</a>). As TDr96_F1 never flowered during the current study period, we could not determine its sex. While <i>D. rotundata</i> is characterized by different ploidy levels (2× and 3×) with a basic chromosome number of 20 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Scarcelli N, Daïnou O, Agbangla C, Tostain S, Pham JL. Segregation patterns of isozyme loci and microsatellite markers show the diploidy of African yam Dioscorea rotundata (2n = 40). Theor Appl Genet. 2005;111:226–32." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d980e1086">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Girma G, Hyma KE, Asiedu R, Mitchell SE, Gedil M, Spillane C. Next-generation sequencing based genotyping, cytometry and phenotyping for understanding diversity and evolution of guinea yams. Theor Appl Genet. 2014;127:1783–94." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d980e1089">9</a>], we found TDr96_F1 to be diploid (2<i>n</i> = 2× = 40) based on the mitotic chromosome number within root meristem cells (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig1">1c</a>). We estimated the genome size of TDr96_F1 to be 570 Mb by flow cytometry (FCM) analysis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig1">1d</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-017-0419-x/MediaObjects/12915_2017_419_Fig1_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-017-0419-x/MediaObjects/12915_2017_419_Fig1_HTML.gif" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Determination of ploidy level and genome size in <i>Dioscorea rotundata</i> plant TDr96_F1. <b>a</b> TDr96_F1 plant grown in a greenhouse at Iwate Biotechnology Research Center (IBRC), Japan. Bar = 50 cm. <b>b</b> TDr96_F1 tuber. Bar = 10 cm. <b>c</b> Diploid somatic chromosomes at metaphase stage obtained from TDr96_F1 root tips (2<i>n</i> = 2× = 40). <b>d</b> FCM histogram of propidium iodide (PI)-stained nuclei from <i>D. rotundata</i> (TDr96_F1) and rice (<i>Oryza sativa</i> L.). Rice (genome size = 380 Mb) served as an internal reference standard. 1 = G1 (<i>O. sativa</i>), 2 = G1 (<i>D. rotundata</i>), and 3 = G2 (<i>D. rotundata</i>), where G1 and G2 represent the Gap 1 and Gap 2 phases of the cell cycle, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>We used total DNA from fresh leaf samples to prepare a paired-end (PE) library and eight types of mate-pair (MP) jump libraries with insert sizes of 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 8, 20, and 40 kb and sequenced the PE and MP jump libraries on Illumina sequencers. We also generated a 100-kb jump bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) library, from which 9984 clones were subjected to BAC-end Sanger sequencing, resulting in PE reads corresponding to a 0.46-Gb sequence with ~ 0.8× genome coverage (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S2 and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S2). In total, we generated 85.14 Gb of sequencing reads, representing ~ 149.4× coverage of the estimated 570-Mb genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S2). Using <i>k</i>-mer analysis-based genome size estimation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Marçais G, Kingsford C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:764–70." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d980e1169">10</a>] of TDr96_F1 PE reads with ALLPATHS-LG [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Gnerre S, Maccallum I, Przybylski D, Ribeiro FJ, Burton JN, Walker BJ, et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2011;108:1513–18." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d980e1173">11</a>] (see below), we found that the genome size was roughly 579 Mb, which is similar to the size estimated by FCM (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S3). The PE and MP jump reads were used for de novo assembly with the ALLPATHS-LG assembler [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Gnerre S, Maccallum I, Przybylski D, Ribeiro FJ, Burton JN, Walker BJ, et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2011;108:1513–18." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d980e1179">11</a>], which provides good performance even for highly heterozygous genomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Earl D, Bradnam K, St John J, Darling A, Lin D, Fass J, et al. Assemblathon 1: a competitive assessment of de novo short read assembly methods. Genome Res. 2011;21:2224–41." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d980e1182">12</a>]. Further scaffolding with SSPACE software using the 100-kb jump reads [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Boetzer M, Henkel CV, Jansen HJ, Butler D, Pirovano W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:578–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d980e1185">13</a>] (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S4) generated 4723 scaffolds with a total length of 594 Mb, i.e., 2.6% and 4.2% longer than the genome size estimated by <i>k</i>-mer (579 Mb) and FCM (570 Mb) analyses, respectively. We estimated the scaffold N50 to be 2.12 Mb (longest scaffold: 13.6 Mb), with approximately 93.9% of the assembly represented by 586 scaffolds longer than 100 kb (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S3). From ALLPATHS-LG output, we judged that more than 1.4 million sites were potentially heterozygous (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Tab1">1</a>). This assembly is hereafter referred to as the “TDr96_F1 reference genome.”</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Characteristics of nuclear genome sequence in <i>Dioscorea rotundata</i> and other angiosperms</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>We assessed the quality of our assembly by investigating the presence of 248 highly conserved core eukaryotic genes with the Core Eukaryotic Genes Mapping Approach (CEGMA) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Parra G, Bradnam K, Korf I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics. 2007;23:1061–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d980e2240">14</a>] and confirmed the presence of 243 (98%) of those genes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S4). Similarly, 94% of 956 Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCOs) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Simão FA, Waterhouse RM, Ioannidis P, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics. 2015;31:3210–12." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d980e2246">15</a>] were present in at least one complete single copy in the assembly (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S5). Since the TDr96_F1 reference genome was generated from total genomic DNA, it also contained organelle-derived sequences. Alignment of the TDr96_F1 PE reads to the published <i>D. rotundata</i> chloroplast genome sequence [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Mariac C, Scarcelli N, Pouzadou J, Barnaud A, Billot C, Faye A, et al. Cost-effective enrichment hybridization capture of chloroplast genomes at deep multiplexing levels for population genetics and phylogeography studies. Mol Ecol Resour. 2014;14:1103–13." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d980e2256">16</a>] showed that 14.7% of the total PE reads were derived from the chloroplast genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S6). We also isolated mitochondrial DNA from TDr96_F1 leaves, sequenced this DNA using PE reads with Illumina MiSeq, and generated a 564-kb de novo assembly comprising 76 scaffolds (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S5). Among PE reads, 1.25% represented mitochondrial sequences.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Generation of pseudo-chromosomes by anchoring scaffolds onto a linkage map</h3><p>We developed a genetic map of <i>D. rotundata</i> using 150 F1 individuals obtained from a cross between two heterozygous breeding lines, TDr97/00917 (P1, female) and TDr99/02627 (P2, male), using restriction site associated DNA (RAD)-tags as DNA markers [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Baird NA, Etter PD, Atwood TS, Currey MC, Shiver AL, Lewis ZA, et al. Rapid SNP discovery and genetic mapping using sequenced RAD markers. PLoS One. 2008;3:e3376." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d980e2276">17</a>] (Additional file 2: Figures S6, S7) and the pseudo-testcross method [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Grattapaglia D, Sederoff R. Genetic linkage maps of Eucalyptus grandis and Eucalyptus urophylla using a pseudo-testcross: mapping strategy and RAPD markers. Genetics. 1994;137:1121–37." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d980e2279">18</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Terauchi R, Kahl G. Mapping of the Dioscorea tokoro genome: AFLP markers linked to sex. Genome. 1999;42:752–62." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d980e2282">19</a>]. We aligned RAD-tags to TDr96_F1 scaffold sequences and selected DNA markers heterozygous in P1 and homozygous in P2, as well as markers heterozygous in P2 and homozygous in P1, resulting in 1326 and 1272 markers for P1 and P2 heterozygous sites, respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S7 and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S8). We then calculated the recombination fraction (<i>rf</i>) between the RAD markers to generate linkage maps. If the pairwise <i>rf</i> value of two RAD markers on the same scaffold exceeded 0.25, the scaffold was divided halfway between the markers because they were likely misassembled (see explanation in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S9 and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S8). Two linkage maps, P1-Map and P2-Map, were generated based on the segregation pattern of the selected markers in the F1 progeny (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S10), to which <i>D. rotundata</i> scaffolds were anchored using the 100-bp DNA sequences of RAD-tags. We combined the two maps using shared scaffolds (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figures S11, S12), which allowed the ~ 454-Mb sequence (representing 76.4% of the assembly) to be anchored onto 21 linkage groups (LGs) to construct chromosome-scale pseudo-molecules (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig2">2</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S9). Smaller LGs could not be unequivocally mapped; hence, 21 LGs were obtained, whereas 20 LGs are expected based on the basic chromosome number. We validated the quality of our assembly by comparing the pseudo-molecule sequence with newly sequenced PE sequence reads having an average insert size of ~ 100 kb obtained from the BACs. Among the 315 BAC clones for which sequences of both ends could be mapped onto the assembly, 265 (84.1%) had both pairs in the same scaffold in the correct orientation, with an average distance of 116 kb (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S10 and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S13), confirming the quality of our assembly. We compared the de novo assembled scaffolds to linkage information about the RAD markers, finding that 75% of RAD markers on the same scaffolds had <i>rf</i> &lt; 0.25, and 73.5% of the scaffolds were retained without the need for splitting (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S11). The remaining 26.5% of the scaffolds had an <i>rf</i> &gt; 0.25 and were divided into two or more scaffolds to solve the inconsistency between assembly and linkage information.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-017-0419-x/MediaObjects/12915_2017_419_Fig2_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-017-0419-x/MediaObjects/12915_2017_419_Fig2_HTML.gif" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Integrated genetic and physical map of <i>D. rotundata</i>. Approximately 76.4% of <i>D. rotundata</i> scaffold sequences were anchored using a RAD-based genetic map generated with 150 F1 individuals obtained from a cross between TDr97/00917 (P1, female) and TDr99/02627 (P2, male). The 21 chromosome-scale pseudo-molecules are numbered from 1 to 21. Markers are located according to genetic distance (cM). <i>Black lines</i> represent the 21 P1 and P2 linkage groups (LGs), and scaffolds anchored to P1 and P2 LGs are shown in <i>red</i> and <i>blue</i>, respectively. Scaffolds shared between the P1 and P2 LGs are shown in <i>green. Numbers</i> and <i>arrows</i> indicate scaffolds and their orientation, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Guinea yam gene prediction and comparative genomics</h3><p>We predicted genes and transposons using the TDr96_F1 reference genome sequence. To construct reliable gene models, we followed the MAKER pipeline using RNA-seq data from 18 samples representing various <i>D. rotundata</i> tissues (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Tables S12, S13) and combined the data with publicly available expressed sequence tags (ESTs) and homologous protein sequences from related angiosperm species (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S14). This resulted in the prediction of 26,198 genes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Tab1">1</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM3">3</a>), 22,477 (85.8%) of which are supported by RNA-seq data.</p><p>We compared Guinea yam genome sequence metrics with those of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (dicot), <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> (monocot), and <i>Oryza sativa</i> (monocot) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Tab1">1</a>). Interestingly, the GC contents of the total genome and exons of protein-coding genes in Guinea yam were 35.8% and 44.1%, respectively; these values are close to those of <i>Arabidopsis</i> and much lower than those of the Poales species <i>Brachypodium</i> and <i>Oryza</i> (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Tab1">1</a>). We annotated an average of 6.03 exons and 4.03 introns per gene. Roughly half of the genome was represented by an interspersed sequence (274.5 Mb), a major component of which was long terminal repeat (LTR) sequences (135.7 Mb) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Tab1">1</a>).</p><p>We identified 5557 <i>D. rotundata</i> genes with a 1:1:1:1 orthologous relationship to the high-quality <i>B. distachyon</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, and <i>A. thaliana</i> gene models (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig3">3a</a> and Additional files <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM4">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM5">5</a>). This number was reduced to 2795 genes when we included Arecales (<i>Elaeis guineensis</i>, <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i>) and Zingiberales (<i>Musa acuminata</i>) in our analysis (Additional files <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM6">6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM7">7</a>). We constructed a phylogenetic tree based on the alignment of 2381 orthologous protein-coding genes in the five monocotyledonous species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig3">3b</a>). <i>D. rotundata</i> did not group with any species in the tree, including <i>Musa</i> of Zingiberales, <i>Phoenix</i> and <i>Elaeis</i> of Arecales, and <i>Oryza</i> and <i>Brachypodium</i> of Poales, suggesting that <i>Dioscorea</i> diversified independently from these taxa in monocotyledons.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-017-0419-x/MediaObjects/12915_2017_419_Fig3_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-017-0419-x/MediaObjects/12915_2017_419_Fig3_HTML.gif" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Comparative genomics of <i>Dioscorea rotundata</i> and other angiosperm species. <b>a</b> Venn diagram showing conserved and unique genes at 1:1 correspondence among <i>D. rotundata</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>, and <i>Oryza sativa.</i> Total gene counts in each genome are given below the species name. <b>b</b> Maximum likelihood tree of <i>D. rotundata</i>, <i>B. distachyon</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>Elaeis guineensis</i>, <i>Musa acuminata</i>, and <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i> based on 2381 orthologous protein-coding genes. The bootstrap values across 1000 resamplings are shown. The scale bar represents the mean number of substitutions per site. <b>c</b> Phylogenetic analysis of the relationships of mannose-specific bulb-type lectin proteins in <i>D. rotundata</i> (<i>red</i>), <i>A. thaliana</i> (<i>blue</i>), <i>B. distachyon</i> (<i>green</i>), and <i>O. sativa</i> (<i>orange</i>). <i>Arrowheads</i> represent bulb-type lectins observed to have enriched expression in tubers. High confidence bootstrap values (1000 replicates) are represented at the nodes of the tree as <i>dots. Thick red</i> and <i>blue lines</i> show two root branches of <i>D. rotundata</i>-specific expanded genes</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>For 12,625 <i>D. rotundata</i> genes, no orthologs or paralogs were found in <i>B. distachyon</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, or <i>A. thaliana</i>, and 11,348 <i>D. rotundata</i> genes had no clear homologs in any of the six species shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig3">3a</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM8">8</a>. Of these 11,348 genes without homologs, 3422 were expressed in tuber tissues, a tissue type not shared with the other species examined.</p><p>Non-redundant Gene Ontology (GO) terms “intracellular organelle”, “protein binding”, and “ion binding” were significantly enriched among <i>D. rotundata</i> genes that showed no orthology to the other species, but not among the conserved genes (Additional files <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM9">9</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM10">10</a>). <i>D. rotundata</i> genes without orthologs in the other species included 68 genes encoding proteins with lectin domains that are involved in defense against microbial pathogens, nematodes, and insects, accounting for 31% of the 216 lectin-coding genes functionally annotated in <i>D. rotundata</i>. Among the 12 subfamilies of lectins [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Jiang SY, Ma Z, Ramachandran S. Evolutionary history and stress regulation of the lectin superfamily in higher plants. BMC Evol Biol. 2010;10:79." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d980e2643">20</a>], the bulb-type lectin (snowdrop lectin; B-lectin) family contributed the largest share (110) of genes in <i>D. rotundata</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S14). Phylogenetic analysis of the B-lectin genes in <i>D. rotundata</i> (110 genes; 51 unique), <i>B. distachyon</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, and <i>A. thaliana</i> revealed two expansions of B-lectin genes in <i>Dioscorea</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig3">3c</a>). The first expansion (blue band) consisted of 22 receptor-like serine/threonine-protein kinases, which are thought to play a role in signaling and the activation of plant defense mechanisms [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Afzal AJ, Wood AJ, Lightfoot DA. Plant receptor-like serine threonine kinases: roles in signaling and plant defense. Mol Plant Microbe Interact. 2008;21:507–17." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d980e2671">21</a>]. The second expansion (red band) consisted of 28 mannose-binding lectins sharing high similarity with <i>Dioscorea batatas</i> tuber lectin DB1 (accession number AB178475). DB1 has insecticidal properties against cotton bollworm (<i>Helicoverpa armigera</i>), and studies in transgenic tobacco and rice plants expressing <i>DB1</i> demonstrated that it also confers resistance against green peach aphid and brown plant hopper, respectively [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ohizumi Y, Gaidamashvili M, Ohwada S, Matsuda K, Kominami J, Nakamura-Tsuruta S, et al. Mannose-binding lectin from yam (Dioscorea batatas) tubers with insecticidal properties against Helicoverpa armigera (Lepidoptera: Noctuidae). J Agric Food Chem. 2009;57:2896–902." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d980e2684">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kato T, Hori M, Ogawa T, Muramoto K, Toriyama K. Expression of gene for Dioscorea batatas tuber lectin 1 in transgenic tobacco confers resistance to green-peach aphid. Plant Biotechnol. 2010;27:141–5." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d980e2684_1">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Yoshimura S, Komatsu M, Kaku K, Hori M, Ogawa T, Muramoto K, et al. Production of transgenic rice plants expressing Dioscorea batatas tuber lectin 1 to confer resistance against brown planthopper. Plant Biotechnol. 2012;29:501–4." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d980e2687">24</a>]. Of these mannose-binding lectin genes in Guinea yam, 16 did not have orthologs in any of the six other species examined, and two showed enriched expression (Benjamini–Hochberg [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Benjamini Y, Hochberg Y. Controlling the false discovery rate: a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J R Stat Soc B Methodol. 1995;57(1):289–300." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d980e2690">25</a>] adjusted <i>P</i> value [padj] &lt; 0.05) in tubers.</p><p>RNA-seq analysis comparing three tuber tissues to all other nine tissues (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S12) revealed that 2023 genes were enriched in tubers. The top 50 highly expressed (padj &lt; 0.05) genes included genes encoding starch synthases and branching enzymes, as well as three carbonic anhydrase-encoding genes. Basic Local Alignment Search Tool (BLASTP) (<a href="https://blast.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/">https://blast.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov</a>) analysis showed that these carbonic anhydrase-encoding genes shared high identity (average 76%) with genes encoding <i>Dioscorea japonica</i> precursors of dioscorin, a tuber storage protein that has carbonic anhydrase activity and exists in multiple isoforms [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Xue YL, Miyakawa T, Sawano Y, Tanokura M. Cloning of genes and enzymatic characterizations of novel dioscorin isoforms from Dioscorea japonica. Plant Sci. 2012;183:14–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d980e2712">26</a>] (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM11">11</a>).</p><p>To infer the past genome duplication in <i>D. rotundata</i>, we performed genome-wide dot plot analysis of <i>D. rotundata</i> against itself (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S15), which revealed no indication of genome duplication. Nevertheless, we observed 946 paralogous gene clusters composed of duplicated genes in <i>D. rotundata</i>. Of these, 145 duplicate clusters of paralogous genes were observed only in <i>D. rotundata</i>. To investigate macrosynteny between <i>D. rotundata</i> and related species, we carried out whole-genome syntenic dot plot analysis against the genomes of <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i>, and <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i>. At the chromosomal level, it was difficult to observe synteny conservation between these species. To assess microsynteny conservation, we performed a syntenic path assembly [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Lyons E, et al. Using genomic sequencing for classical genetics in E. coli K12. PLoS One. 2011;6(2):e16717." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d980e2749">27</a>] of the scaffolds from these species against <i>D. rotundata-</i>masked pseudo-chromosomes (see Methods). The reordering and reorientation of the scaffolds relative to <i>D. rotundata</i> pseudo-molecules identified large proportions of the genomes to be conserved at the microsyntenic level (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S16). This suggested that the <i>D. rotundata</i> genome has undergone many recombination events after its divergence from the other species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Whole-genome resequencing of F1 bulk segregants identifies a genomic region associated with sex determination in <i>D. rotundata</i>
                        </h3><p>We previously developed a next generation sequencing (NGS)-based method for bulked segregant analysis (BSA) for quantitative trait locus (QTL) mapping in rice, named QTL-seq [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Takagi H, Abe A, Yoshida K, Kosugi S, Natsume S, Mitsuoka C, et al. QTL-seq: rapid mapping of quantitative trait loci in rice by whole genome resequencing of DNA from two bulked populations. Plant J. 2013;74:174–83." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d980e2777">28</a>]. To our knowledge, this method has not been applied in species with highly heterozygous genomes. The majority of <i>Dioscorea</i> species, including <i>D. rotundata</i>, are mostly dioecious, with separate male and female plants (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig4">4a</a>), making it interesting to understand the genetic mechanism of sex determination in this genus. From a cross between two <i>D. rotundata</i> breeding accessions, TDr97/00917 (P3, female) and TDr97/00777 (P4, male), we generated an F1 population of 253 individuals in 2014 that segregated for male, female, monoecious (male and female flowers on the same plant), and non-flowering types (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S15). For QTL-seq analysis (see Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S17 for details), we sequenced two DNA bulks representing male and female plants, each from 50 individuals, generating 7.9- and 7.3-Gb sequences, which provided 13.9× and 12.7× coverage of the predicted <i>D. rotundata</i> genome, respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S16). We also resequenced the genome of the female parent (P3) and generated a P3 reference sequence (P3-Ref) by replacing TDr96_F1 nucleotides with P3 nucleotides at all different sites between the two genotypes. Likewise, we generated the male parent (P4) reference sequence (P4-Ref) by aligning P4 sequence reads to TDr96_F1 and replacing TDr96_F1 nucleotides with those of P4 at all different sites. We then separately aligned sequence reads obtained from F1 male-bulk and female-bulk DNA to the P3- and P4-Ref sequences. To identify single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) markers associated with the F1 gender phenotype, thus potentially suggesting candidate sex-determining gene(s), we focused on SNPs that segregated in the F1 progeny either as SNPs homozygous in the female parent (P3) but heterozygous in the male parent (P4), or vice versa. We could then identify genomic regions with SNPs heterozygous in one parent whose alleles were differentially transmitted to the two sexes in the F1, suggesting Y or W linkage, respectively. This is similar to mapping by backcrossing, but does not require a BC1 generation using inbred lines. Scanning the entire genome identified a single region, from 0.65 Mb to 2.35 Mb on pseudo-chromosome 11, whose SNP-index values (the frequency of short reads aligned to a particular position of the genome with SNPs different from the reference sequence [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Takagi H, Abe A, Yoshida K, Kosugi S, Natsume S, Mitsuoka C, et al. QTL-seq: rapid mapping of quantitative trait loci in rice by whole genome resequencing of DNA from two bulked populations. Plant J. 2013;74:174–83." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d980e2805">28</a>]) differed for male and female bulks in the second category of SNPs just described (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig4">4b</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S18).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-017-0419-x/MediaObjects/12915_2017_419_Fig4_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-017-0419-x/MediaObjects/12915_2017_419_Fig4_HTML.gif" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>QTL-seq-based analysis of sex determination in <i>D. rotundata</i>. <b>a</b> Male and female inflorescences of <i>D. rotundata</i>. Bars = 10 mm. <b>b</b> SNP-index and ∆SNP-index plots generated for pseudo-chromosome 11 (see Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig2">2</a>). DNA samples from 50 male and 50 female F1 individuals were pooled to prepare the male and female bulks, respectively. <i>Green</i>, <i>yellow</i>, and <i>blue dots</i> represent SNP-index values at all SNP positions, and <i>red lines</i> denote the sliding window average SNP-index values at 1-Mb intervals with 50-kb increments. <i>Horizontal brown lines</i> in the ∆SNP-index plot represent the 95% confidence limit. The candidate genomic region presumably associated with sex determination is indicated by a <i>pink background</i>. <b>c</b> Schematic diagram showing the possible genotypes of female (P3, TDr97/00917) and male (P4, TDr97/00777) parents as well as their F1 progeny segregating for female and male. Genotypes of sex-determination locus are indicated as ZW or ZZ. The position of the cleaved amplified polymorphic sequence (CAPS) marker, sp1, is indicated by a <i>dashed line</i>. Sister chromatids are indicated by numbers</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>We identified a sex-linked region with category 2 SNP markers that are heterozygous in the female parent (P3) but homozygous in the male parent (P4) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S18), suggesting that the male sex is determined by the homozygous (designated ZZ) state of the locus responsible for sex determination, whereas that of the female sex is determined by the heterozygous (ZW) (or hemizygous: Z-) state of this locus (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig4">4c</a>). Genotyping of the F1 individuals used for bulk sequencing using the cleaved amplified polymorphic sequence (CAPS) marker sp1 developed within the candidate genomic region revealed significant co-segregation between the sp1 marker and the sex of the individual (<i>P</i> = 1.913e-14, Fisher’s exact test). This analysis confirmed that the genomic region identified by QTL-seq is indeed associated with sex determination (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig5">5a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig5">b</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S19). The switch of sp1 male and female marker genotypes in the F1 progeny occurred because the marker genotype was heterozygous in the female parent (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig4">4c</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-017-0419-x/MediaObjects/12915_2017_419_Fig5_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-017-0419-x/MediaObjects/12915_2017_419_Fig5_HTML.gif" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>A CAPS marker developed on pseudo-chromosome 11 co-segregates with sex in F1 progeny derived from a cross between female (P3) and male (P4) parents. <b>a</b> Agarose gel electrophoresis of the CAPS marker, sp1, for the parents and F1 progeny segregating for male and female phenotypes. This marker segregates for a non-cleaved band (854 bp) indicated as (A) and cleaved bands (425 bp + 428 bp) indicated as (B). <b>b</b> Frequency of the sp1 genotypes (A/B or A/A) among the F1 progeny segregating for male (50 plants) and female (50 plants). There is a statistically significant association between A/B sp1 genotype and male and between A/A sp1 genotype and female (Fisher’s exact test: <i>P</i> = 1.913e-14)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>As the TDr96_F1 plant never flowered, we were unable to determine its sex based on flower phenotype and therefore could not directly characterize its genotype (ZZ or ZW) at the candidate sex locus. To identify the genomic regions linked to Z and W, we assembled the P3 (female) and P4 (male) genomes de novo using their PE reads with the DISCOVAR De Novo assembler [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Love RR, et al. Evaluation of DISCOVAR de novo using a mosquito sample for cost-effective short-read genome assembly. BMC Genomics. 2016;17(1):187." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d980e2931">29</a>], generating P3-DDN (female, N50 = 3.3 kb) and P4-DDN (male, N50 = 2.7 kb) reference sequences (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig6">6a</a>, Additional file 1: Table S17 and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S20). We separately mapped short reads derived from bulked DNA from 50 male and 50 female F1 progeny (P3 × P4) to P3-DDN and P4-DDN and looked for unique P3-DDN (female) genomic regions (presumably corresponding to the W-linked region) that were specifically mapped by F1 female-bulk reads but not by F1 male-bulk reads. The 1345 regions (sizes from 1 to 129 bp) totaling 15,390 bp conformed to this pattern (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S21). We ordered these fragments by size and found that the N20 value was 42 bp. Conversely, we found only 435 regions (total size 3775 bp) of P4-DDN (male) mapped by the F1-male bulk but not by the F1-female bulk (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S21). The large size difference between female-specific P3-DDN regions (total 15,390 bp) and male-specific P4-DDN regions (total 3775 bp) suggested that the ZW female genome has additional DNA sequences not present in the ZZ male. We hypothesize that the recovery of small male-specific P4-DDN regions may have occurred by chance. We focused on 36 female-specific contigs of P3-DDN that contained DNA fragments larger than 42 bp (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig6">6b</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S21). When we used the 36 contigs as BLASTN queries against the TDr96_F1 reference genome, 20 were located on scaffold206 (667.8 kb) on pseudo-chromosome 11 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig6">6c</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S18), suggesting that P3-DDN contigs with female-specific regions were indeed located within the sex-linked region identified by QTL-seq (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig4">4b</a>). We developed a PCR primer pair for one such P3-DDN contig (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig6">6b</a>; Female917_flattened_line_87512_3057) harboring female-specific regions; we named this DNA marker sp16. sp16 amplified a PCR fragment in the P3 female parent but not in the P4 male parent (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig7">7a</a>), demonstrating that this fragment was located in the female-specific region. An sp16 PCR fragment was amplified in TDr96_F1, our reference genome plant (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig7">7a</a>), suggesting that this individual likely had the ZW genotype. In F1 progeny derived from a P3 × P4 cross, the sp16 fragment was amplified in all female plants, but it failed to be amplified in the majority of male individuals. Furthermore, sp16 fragments were amplified in monoecious as well as non-flowering progeny (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig7">7a</a>). We monitored flowering in all 249 F1 individuals in two consecutive seasons (2014 and 2015) and found that 194 plants showed consistent sex phenotypes. However, the remaining 55 plants showed changes in sex among male, female, and monoecious (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig7">7b</a>). Genotyping of all F1 individuals using sp16 revealed a striking pattern: 121 of the 125 plants that were consistent for male over the 2 years showed no PCR amplification of sp16, whereas all plants with the remaining phenotypes showed amplification of sp16 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig7">7b</a>). A similar pattern was observed in another F1 family (TDr04-219 × P4) involving the same male parent, P4 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig7">7c</a>). We also assayed 24 Guinea yam breeding accessions of known sex using the same marker (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig8">8</a>). All 10 female accessions, as well as three accessions that did not flower, showed amplification of sp16. Of the 11 male accessions genotyped, eight did not show amplification of sp16, whereas the remaining three did.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-017-0419-x/MediaObjects/12915_2017_419_Fig6_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-017-0419-x/MediaObjects/12915_2017_419_Fig6_HTML.gif" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Identification of female-specific putative W-linked genomic region. <b>a</b> Schematic diagram of the method used to identify the female-specific putative W-linked genomic region. De novo assembled genome sequences of female (P3-DDN) and male (P4-DDN) parents were combined to serve as a reference sequence. Short reads of bulked DNA from F1 female and F1 male progeny were separately mapped onto this combined reference sequence. The majority of reads mapped to two duplicated homologous locations in the reference genome (indicated as “common regions”), which gave low MAPQ scores (&lt;60) in the BWA alignment. Female parental contigs that were mapped only with reads belonging to the F1 female bulk corresponded to female-specific genomic regions. Sequence reads mapped to such positions were identified by their high MAPQ scores (=60). <b>b</b> An example of a female-specific contig (contig Female917_flattened_line_87512_3057). Alignment depths of F1 female bulk (<i>red</i>) and F1 male bulk (<i>blue</i>) are shown (<i>top</i>). Frequency of reads mapped with MAPQ score = 60. The <i>red line</i> corresponds to genomic regions that were covered by short reads, &gt; 90% of which had a MAPQ score of 60 (middle). A genomic region that is covered only by female reads (not by male reads) and &gt; 90% of mapped reads had MAPQ score = 60 (indicated by <i>gray bars</i>) (<i>bottom</i>). <i>Red arrowheads</i> indicate the positions of PCR primers for the DNA marker sp16. <b>c</b> Location of the female-specific genomic region. <i>Thick gray horizontal line</i> denotes pseudo-chromosome 11 (<i>top</i>), scaffolds on chromosome 11 (<i>middle</i>), and scaffold206 (<i>bottom</i>). The <i>thin blue lines</i> shown under the <i>first</i>, <i>second</i>, and <i>third horizontal lines</i> indicate the positions of female contigs (P3-DDN) specifically mapped by F1 female bulk reads. The <i>square box</i> at the bottom indicates alignment depth of reads of F1 female bulk (<i>red</i>) and F1 bulk of progeny in which sp16 amplification was not observed (sp16-minus) (<i>blue</i>) to scaffold206. <i>Red triangles</i> indicate the position of DNA marker sp16</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                           <div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Fig. 7"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-017-0419-x/MediaObjects/12915_2017_419_Fig7_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-017-0419-x/MediaObjects/12915_2017_419_Fig7_HTML.gif" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>DNA marker sp16 is located in a W-linked region. <b>a</b> Results of agarose gel electrophoresis of PCR products amplified by DNA marker sp16 (sp16). <i>Actin</i> from <i>D. rotundata</i> (Dr-Actin) served as a control to show that template DNA was present for all samples. <i>NF</i> non-flowering. <b>b</b> Bar graphs showing the correspondence of sp16 genotypes (sp16 PCR product Amplified or Not amplified) with the sex of F1 progeny derived from a cross between P3 and P4 and phenotyped over 2 years (2014 and 2015). Color codes indicate sex manifestation of the plants during the 2-year period, disregarding the yearly order (i.e., plants showing sex changes from male [2014] to female [2015] and female [2014] to male [2015] were combined and are indicated by ♀/♂). Monoecy is indicated by (♀/♂). <i>NF</i> non-flowering. <b>c</b> The same as <b>b</b> but for F1 progeny obtained from a separate cross involving TDr04-219 and P4</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                           <div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="Fig. 8"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 8</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/figures/8" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-017-0419-x/MediaObjects/12915_2017_419_Fig8_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-8-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-017-0419-x/MediaObjects/12915_2017_419_Fig8_HTML.gif" alt="figure8" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-8-desc"><p>Test of correspondence between sp16 genotypes and sex in 24 <i>D. rotundata</i> breeding accessions. Results of agarose gel electrophoresis of PCR products amplified using sp16 DNA markers are shown. Dr-Actin is a control indicating the presence of template DNA for all lines. <i>NF</i> non-flowering</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x/figures/8" data-track-dest="link:Figure8 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>We hypothesized that the ZZ genotype stably gives rise to the male phenotype, whereas the ZW genotype results in unstable sex phenotypes; ZW mainly generates the female phenotype, but sometimes monoecious or male phenotypes depending on the environments. Therefore, some individuals of the F1 progeny derived from a cross between P3 and P4 might have been scored as male despite their genotype being ZW, which may have obscured our analysis, resulting in non-zero depth of male DNA bulk within the putative W-region (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig6">6b</a>). To address this possibility, we selected 50 ZZ plants from the F1 progeny based on their sp16 genotype and bulked and sequenced the DNA (sp16-minus bulk). The sp16-minus bulk reads, as well as female bulk reads, were separately mapped to the combined sequence of the TDr96_F1 reference genome and P4-DDN to identify the female-specific TDr96_F1 genomic region, as described in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig6">6a</a>.
