As shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig6">6c</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S20c, d, we successfully delineated the putative W-linked region mapped predominantly with female-only bulk DNA, representing an approximately 161-kb region of scaffold206 on pseudo-chromosome 11. This putative female-specific W-linked region contains ~ 10 predicted genes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM12">12</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>Molecular markers, such as simple sequence repeats (SSRs), indels, and SNPs, can, for the first time, be developed for various applications in Guinea yam, including linkage mapping, genome-wide association analysis, genomic selection, and MAS. We have already analyzed sequences containing SSR motifs in the genome and identified more than 22,000 candidates that can be used to design primers (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S19). We designed primer pairs for 1000 of these sequences and obtained the information necessary for their immediate use in genetic analyses (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM13">13</a>). SSR markers isolated from one <i>Dioscorea</i> species can be transferred to other species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Tamiru M, Yamanaka S, Mitsuoka C, Babil P, Takagi H, Lopez-Montes A, et al. Development of genomic simple sequence repeat markers for Yam. Crop Sci. 2015;55:2191–200." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d980e3181">30</a>]. From a practical plant breeding point of view, the sp16 sex-linked marker should prove useful for selecting plantlets for crossing, substantially saving the space and labor required to grow plants and accelerating breeding programs. However, the sex-determination system may vary among <i>Dioscorea</i> species (see below), so the transferability of sex-linked DNA markers from <i>D. rotundata</i> to other species should be addressed in future studies.</p><p>Our identification of the locus underpinning an important trait by QTL-seq, using F1 progeny derived from highly heterozygous parents, opens up new avenues to WGS-based mapping of important traits in crops and tree species for which inbred lines are difficult to obtain and/or generation times are too long, impeding the use of conventional linkage analysis approaches.</p><p>Development of DNA markers linked to agronomically important traits and their use for MAS increase the role yam plays in ensuring food security for resource-poor households in Africa and beyond. The <i>D. rotundata</i> genome sequences reported here should also contribute to understanding the origin of Guinea yam and its domestication from its wild progenitor species, which are widely distributed in West and Central Africa.</p><p>Our results suggest that the Guinea yam sex-determination system involves female heterogamy (male = ZZ, female = ZW). We identified two DNA markers, sp1 (linked to the putative Z-linked region) and sp16 (presumably located within the putative W-linked region, which in TDr96_F1 is presumed to be ZW, and spans only 161 kb). The chromosomes carrying the Z- and W-linked regions are probably not strongly differentiated, and diverged sequences corresponding to Z and W chromosomes were not recovered in our reference genome. Future work should test for structural differences, such as inversions, between the Z- and W-linked regions. Guinea yam sex determination is not, however, a simple genetic system. The consistent maleness of individuals with the ZZ genotype, based on the sp16 sequence, versus occasional maleness of ZW individuals, suggests that maleness is the default phenotype and that the W allele is dominant over Z and can, but does not always, suppress male organ development and feminize the flower. If the feminizing function of the W allele fails in a subset of flowers, the individual will be monoecious. ZW individuals can change sex over time (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig7">7</a>), indicating that the Z-suppressing function can be affected by the environment. Self-pollination between male and female flowers of ZW monoecious plants could become possible, which may allow inbred lines to be generated, allowing fixation of desired alleles of agronomically important traits. To make it practical, though, we may have to carefully monitor the level of inbreeding depression in <i>D. rotundata</i>. Dioecy is the norm in <i>Dioscorea</i> species, and previous reports suggest that males are usually the heterogametic sex (XY) in the genus [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Martin FW. Sex ratio and sex determination in Dioscorea. J Heredity. 1966;57:96–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d980e3212">31</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Terauchi R, Kahl G. Sex determination in Dioscorea tokoro, a wild yam species. In: Ainsworth CC, editor. Sex determination in plants. Oxford: BIOS Scientific Publishers; 1999. p. 163–71." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d980e3215">32</a>]. A genetic study of <i>D. tokoro</i> also confirmed an XY male system [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Terauchi R, Kahl G. Mapping of the Dioscorea tokoro genome: AFLP markers linked to sex. Genome. 1999;42:752–62." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d980e3222">19</a>]. <i>D. tokoro</i> belongs to the section Stenophora, which is distantly related to the section Enantiophyllum, which contains <i>D. rotundata</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Maurin O, Muasya M, Catalan P, Shongwe EZ, Viruel J, Wilkin P, van der Bank M. Diversification into novel habitats in the Africa clade of Dioscorea (Dioscoreaceae): erect habit and elephant’s foot tubers. BMC Evol Biol. 2016;16:238." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d980e3231">3</a>]. Our data suggest that the sex-determination system has changed within the genus during the evolution, which could be an interesting topic for future studies. Once the <i>D. rotundata</i> sex-determination gene has been isolated, its comparison with another dioecious monocot species such as <i>Asparagus</i>, for which the sex-determination gene has been recently isolated [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Murase K, Shigenobu S, Fujii S, Ueda K, Murata T, et al. MYB transcription factor gene involved in sex determination in Asparagus officinalis. Genes Cells. 2016;22:115–23." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d980e3241">33</a>], would be interesting.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>Here, we sequenced the whole genome (594 Mb) of the dioecious tuber crop Guinea yam (<i>Dioscorea rotundata</i>) using a heterozygous individual and anchored the scaffolds to 21 linkage groups to generate pseudo-chromosomes. We exploited the genome sequence to map the sex-determination locus by QTL-seq using BSA of F1 progeny. This analysis revealed a genomic region on pseudo-chromosome 11 tightly linked to femaleness within a female heterogametic (ZZ = male, ZW = female) sex-determination system. This genome sequence will serve as a springboard towards gene mapping and discovery in yam (<i>Dioscorea</i> spp.) and genetic improvement of these important yet neglected staple crops.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Plant materials</h3><p>The TDr96_F1 line used for WGS was selected from F1 progeny obtained from an open-pollinated <i>D. rotundata</i> breeding line (TDr96/00629) grown under field conditions in the experimental fields of the International Institute of Tropical Agriculture (IITA) in Nigeria. F1 seeds from TDr96/00629 and those obtained from the cross between the parental lines TDr97/00917 and TDr99/02627 used for RAD-seq were germinated on wet paper towels in darkness at 28 °C. After germination, the seeds were transferred to soil (Sakata Supermix A [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Sakata Seed Co. &#xA;                    http://www.sakataseed.co.jp/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 1 Aug 2017." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d980e3273">34</a>]) and grown at 30 °C with a 16-h/8-h photoperiod in a greenhouse at Iwate Biotechnology Research Institute (IBRC) in Japan. Fresh leaf samples were collected for DNA extraction. Additionally, to resequence the F1 progeny used for QTL-seq analysis, lyophilized leaf samples obtained from plants that were grown and phenotyped under field conditions at IITA were used for DNA extraction.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Determination of chromosome number and ploidy level</h3><p>For chromosome observation, root tips of TDr96_F1 plants generated by in vitro propagation of nodal explants were sampled and fixed in acetic acid-alcohol (1:3 ratio) for 24 h without pretreatment. Fixed root tips were stained with a 1% aceto-carmine solution for 24 h. Samples were prepared by the squash method and analyzed under an Olympus BX50 optical microscope (Olympus Optical Co, Ltd., Tokyo, Japan [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Olympus Co. &#xA;                    http://www.olympus-global.com/en/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 1 Aug 2017." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d980e3284">35</a>]) at 400× magnification.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Estimation of <i>D. rotundata</i> genome size</h3><p>The genome size of TDr96_F1 (<i>D. rotundata</i>) was estimated both by FCM and <i>k</i>-mer analyses. FCM analysis was carried out using nuclei prepared from fresh leaf samples of TDr96_F1 and a <i>japonica</i> rice (<i>Oryza sativa</i> L.) cultivar of known genome size (~380 Mb [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature. 2005;436:793–800." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d980e3311">36</a>]), which served as an internal reference standard. Nuclei were isolated and stained with propidium iodide (PI) simultaneously and analyzed using a Cell Lab Quanta™ SC Flow Cytometer (Beckman Coulter, CA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Beckman Coulter Co. &#xA;                    https://www.beckmancoulter.com/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 1 Aug 2017." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d980e3315">37</a>]) following the manufacturer’s protocol. The ratio of G1 peak means [yam (281.7):rice (188.7) = 1.493] was used to estimate the genome size of <i>D. rotundata</i> to be ~ 570 Mb (380 Mb × 1.5). <i>k</i>-mer analysis-based genome size estimation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Marçais G, Kingsford C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:764–70." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d980e3324">10</a>] was performed with TDr96_F1 PE reads with an average size of ~ 230 bp and a total length of 16.77 Gb (16,771,579,510 bp) using ALLPATHS-LG [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Gnerre S, Maccallum I, Przybylski D, Ribeiro FJ, Burton JN, Walker BJ, et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2011;108:1513–18." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d980e3327">11</a>]. <i>k</i>-mer frequency analysis, with the <i>k-</i>mer size set to 25, generated values for <i>k</i>-mer coverage (Kc = 25.66) and mean read length (Rl = 228.8), which were used to estimate the genome size of TDr96_F1 to 579 Mb as follows:</p><div id="Equa" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$ \mathrm{Genome}\kern0.17em \mathrm{Size}=\mathrm{Total}\;\mathrm{PE}\;\mathrm{read}\kern0.17em \mathrm{length}\;\left(\mathrm{bp}\right)\div \mathrm{Read}\kern0.17em \mathrm{coverage}\;\left(\mathrm{Rc}\right) $$</span></div></div>
                           <div id="Equb" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$ \mathrm{Read}\kern0.17em \mathrm{coverage}\;\left(\mathrm{Rc}\right)=\left[k- mer\;\mathrm{coverage}\;\left(\mathrm{Kc}\right)\times \mathrm{Rl}\left]\div \right[\mathrm{Rl}\hbox{--} k\hbox{--} mer\;\mathrm{length}\;\left(\mathrm{k}\right)+1\right] $$</span></div></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Whole-genome sequencing</h3><p>For WGS, genomic DNA was extracted from fresh TDr96_F1 leaf samples using a NucleoSpin Plant II Kit according to the manufacturer’s protocol (Macherey-Nagel GmbH &amp; Co. KG [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Macherey-Nagel GmbH &amp; Co. KG. &#xA;                    http://www.mn-net.com&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 1 Aug 2017." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d980e3562">38</a>]) with slight modifications. Homogenized samples were washed with 0.1 M 4-(2-hydroxyethyl)-1-piperazineethanesulfonic acid (HEPES) buffer to remove contaminating polysaccharides. Just before use, 120 mg polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP), 90 mg <span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-ascorbic acid, and 200 μl 2-mercaptoethanol (ME) were added to 10 ml HEPES buffer, and 1 ml of the mixture was used to wash each sample; washing was repeated three times. Additionally, 10 μl 2-ME and 5 μl of 30% polyethylene glycol (PEG)-20000 were added to 1 ml of PL1 buffer (provided with the NucleoSpin Plant II Kit), and twice the recommended volume of buffer (800 μl) was used for cell lysis. Libraries for PE short reads and MP jump reads of various insert sizes including 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, and 8 kb were constructed using an Illumina TruSeq DNA LT Sample Prep Kit and a Nextera Mate Pair Sample Prep Kit, respectively. The PE library was sequenced on the Illumina MiSeq platform, while the MP libraries were sequenced on the HiSeq 2500 platform. Library construction and sequencing of the 20- and 40-kb MP jump sequences were carried out by Eurofins Genomics (Operon [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Operon Co. &#xA;                    http://www.operon.com/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 1 Aug 2017." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d980e3568">39</a>]) and Lucigen [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Lucigen Co. &#xA;                    http://www.lucigen.com/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 1 Aug 2017." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d980e3571">40</a>], respectively. The 20-kb and 40-kb jump libraries were sequenced on the MiSeq and HiSeq 2500 platforms, respectively. BAC libraries were constructed by Lucigen, and BAC-end sequencing was carried out by Genaris [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Genaris Co. &#xA;                    http://genebay.co.jp/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 11 Sept 2017." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d980e3574">41</a>] using Sanger sequencing. A total of 30,750 clones corresponding to 3072 Mb of sequence and 5.4× genome coverage were constructed. Of these, 9984 clones were used for BAC-end sequencing, generating a 13.6-Mb sequence in PE fasta format, which was converted to 50-bp PE short reads corresponding to a 0.46-Gb sequence and ~ 0.8× coverage of the estimated 470-Mb <i>D. rotundata</i> genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S2 and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S2).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">De novo assembly</h3><p>All TDr96_F1 sequence reads in fastq format were filtered for quality using the FASTX-Toolkit version 0.0.13 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Hannon laboratory. &#xA;                    http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 1 Aug 2017." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d980e3596">42</a>]. For PE reads and MP short jump reads with insert sizes ranging from 2 to 8 kb, only those having sequence reads with a Phred quality score of ≥ 30 (i.e., ≥ 90% of the reads) were retained. Adapter trimming and removal of MP reads with the wrong insert sizes were performed using an in-house pipeline of scripts written in Perl and C++. Quality filtering of the long jump sequences (20-, 40-, and 100-kb insert sizes) was carried out by the suppliers. For the initial de novo assembly, short PE reads and MP jump reads with 2- to 40-kb insert sizes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S2) were assembled using ALLPATHS-LG assembler version R49856 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Gnerre S, Maccallum I, Przybylski D, Ribeiro FJ, Burton JN, Walker BJ, et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2011;108:1513–18." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d980e3602">11</a>]. Further scaffolding of the assembly generated by ALLPATHS-LG was performed using the 100-kb jump MP fastq reads obtained by BAC-end sequencing and the SSPACE PREMIUM 2.3 scaffolding tool with default parameters [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Boetzer M, Henkel CV, Jansen HJ, Butler D, Pirovano W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:578–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d980e3605">13</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Constructing organelle genome sequences</h3><p>De novo assembly of the <i>D. rotundata</i> mitochondrial genome sequence was performed using mitochondrial DNA isolated from TDr96_F1 leaf samples according to the method of Terachi and Tsunewaki [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Terachi T, Tsunewaki K. The molecular basis of genetic diversity among cytoplasms of Triticum and Aegilops: 5. Mitochondrial genome diversity among Aegilops species having identical chloroplast genomes. Theor Appl Genet. 1986;73:175–81." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d980e3619">43</a>] with the following minor modifications. Fresh green leaves (ca. 150 g) were homogenized in 1.5 L of homogenization buffer containing 0.44 M mannitol, 50 mM Tris-HCl (pH 8.0), 3 mM ethylenediaminetetraacetic acid (EDTA), 5 mM 2-ME, 0.1% (w/v) bovine serum albumin, and 0.1% (w/v) PVP. Following DNaseI treatment, the mitochondrial fraction was collected from the interface between 1.30 M and 1.45 M of a sucrose gradient. Mitochondrial DNA was purified by EtBr/CsCl centrifugation at 80,000 rpm for 6 h at 20 °C in a Beckman TLA 100.3 rotor. The DNA band was collected and purified by ethanol precipitation. The resulting mitochondrial DNA (15 ng) was amplified using a REPLI-g Mini Kit (Qiagen, Cat. no. 150023) and used for library construction. The library was sequenced on an Illumina MiSeq sequencer, and the resulting PE reads were assembled de novo using DISCOVAR De Novo [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Love RR, et al. Evaluation of DISCOVAR de novo using a mosquito sample for cost-effective short-read genome assembly. BMC Genomics. 2016;17(1):187." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d980e3622">29</a>], generating <i>D. rotundata</i> mitochondria contigs. For scaffolding, MP reads with insert sizes of 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 8, and 20 kb obtained from <i>D. rotundata</i> genomic DNA (gDNA) were aligned to the <i>D. rotundata</i> mitochondrial contigs. MP reads showing 100% alignment were selected and used for scaffolding of <i>D. rotundata</i> mitochondrial contigs by SSPACE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Boetzer M, Henkel CV, Jansen HJ, Butler D, Pirovano W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:578–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d980e3638">13</a>] (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S5). To reconstruct the <i>D. rotundata</i> chloroplast genome sequence, the PE reads of TDr96_F1 were aligned to the recently published <i>D. rotundata</i> chloroplast genome sequence [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Mariac C, Scarcelli N, Pouzadou J, Barnaud A, Billot C, Faye A, et al. Cost-effective enrichment hybridization capture of chloroplast genomes at deep multiplexing levels for population genetics and phylogeography studies. Mol Ecol Resour. 2014;14:1103–13." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d980e3651">16</a>] (GenBank ID = NC_024170.1) by Burrows-Wheeler alignment (BWA) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Li H, Durbin R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics. 2010;26:589–95." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d980e3654">44</a>], and chloroplast-derived sequences were identified, amounting to 5,403,420 reads (14.74% of the total size of PE reads generated for TDr96_F1 [Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Tab1">1</a>]) matching the assembled 155.4-kb chloroplast genome of <i>D. rotundata</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Evaluation of the completeness of the genomic assembly</h3><p>To evaluate the completeness of the <i>D. rotundata</i> genome assembly, the assembly was checked for the presence of 248 highly conserved core eukaryotic genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Ian Korf Lab. &#xA;                    http://korflab.ucdavis.edu/datasets/genome_completeness/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 1 Aug 2017." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d980e3674">45</a>] using CEGMA version 2.4 with default parameters [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Parra G, Bradnam K, Korf I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics. 2007;23:1061–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d980e3677">14</a>] (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S4). To further assess the completeness of the genome, the successor to CEGMA, Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO), was used to check for the presence of 956 BUSCOs with version 1.1.b1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Simão FA, Waterhouse RM, Ioannidis P, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics. 2015;31:3210–12." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d980e3683">15</a>] using the early access plant dataset (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S5).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Annotation of transposable elements (TEs)</h3><p>Legacy repetitive sequences, including transposons, were predicted using CENSOR 4.2.29 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Kohany O, Gentles AJ, Hankus L, Jurka J. Annotation, submission and screening of repetitive elements in Repbase: RepbaseSubmitter and Censor. BMC Bioinformatics. 2006;7:474." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d980e3698">46</a>] with the following options: show_simple, nofilter, and mode rough using the Munich Information Center for Protein Sequences (MIPS) Repeat Element Database [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Nussbaumer T, Martis MM, Roessner SK, Pfeifer M, Bader KC, Sharma S, et al. MIPS PlantsDB: a database framework for comparative plant genome research. Nucleic Acids Res. 2013;41:D1144–51." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d980e3701">47</a>]. Following identification, the repeat elements were classified using mips-REcat [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Nussbaumer T, Martis MM, Roessner SK, Pfeifer M, Bader KC, Sharma S, et al. MIPS PlantsDB: a database framework for comparative plant genome research. Nucleic Acids Res. 2013;41:D1144–51." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d980e3704">47</a>]. Repetitive sequences were later improved by remodeling using RepeatModeler 1.0.8 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Smit AFA, Hubley R. RepeatModeler. Open-1.0. (2008–2015)." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d980e3707">48</a>] and masked with RepeatMasker 4.0.5 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Smit AFA, Hubley R, Green P. RepeatMasker. Open-4.0. (2013–2015)." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d980e3710">49</a>]. Using the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) database, one of three other options was used to generate interspersed RepeatModeler-based, interspersed Rebase-based, and Low complexity repeats: “nolow”, “nolow, species Viridiplantae”, and “noint”, respectively. Repeat element content and other statistics were compared between the <i>D. rotundata</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> TAIR10 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Lamesch P, Berardini TZ, Li D, Swarbreck D, Wilks C, Sasidharan R, et al. The Arabidopsis Information Resource (TAIR): improved gene annotation and new tools. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012;40:D1202–10." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d980e3720">50</a>], <i>B. distachyon</i> v3.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature. 2010;463:763–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d980e3726">51</a>], and <i>O. sativa</i> v7_JGI 323 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Ouyang S, Zhu W, Hamilton J, Lin H, Campbell M, Childs K, et al. The TIGR Rice Genome Annotation Resource: improvements and new features. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007;35:D883–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d980e3733">52</a>] genomes using the RepeatModeled and RepeatMasked references (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Tab1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">RNA-seq</h3><p>Total RNA was extracted using leaf, stem, flower, and tuber samples collected from a greenhouse-grown TDr96_F1 plant using a Plant RNeasy Kit (Qiagen [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Quiagen Co. &#xA;                    https://www.qiagen.com&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 1 Aug 2017." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d980e3747">53</a>]) with slight modifications. RLC buffer was used for lysis after the addition of 5 μl 30% PEG-20000 and 10 μl 2-ME to 1 ml of buffer. The RNA samples were treated with DNase (Qiagen) to remove contaminating genomic DNA. Two micrograms of total RNA was used to construct complementary DNA (cDNA) libraries using a TruSeq RNA Sample Prep Kit V2 (Illumina) according to the manufacturer’s instructions. The libraries were used for PE sequencing using 2× 100 cycles on the HiSeq 2500 platform in high-output mode. Illumina sequencing reads were filtered by Phred quality score, and reads with a quality score of ≥ 30 (≥90% of reads) were retained (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S12). Only one RNA-seq experiment was carried out per tissue/organ (indicated as sample in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S12).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Prediction of protein-coding genes</h3><p>The legacy gene models were generated previously using the legacy repeat-masked reference genome and three approaches: ab initio, ab initio supported by evidence-based prediction, and evidence-based prediction. The ab initio prediction was carried out with FGENESH 3.1.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Salamov AA, Solovyev VV. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 2000;10:516–22." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d980e3764">54</a>]. The ab initio supported by evidence-based prediction was performed with AUGUSTUS 3.0.3 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Stanke M, Steinkamp R, Waack S, Morgenstern B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2004;32:W309–12." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d980e3767">55</a>] using the maize5 training set and a hint file as the gene model support information. To construct the hint file, TopHat 2.0.11 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Kim D, Pertea G, Trapnell C, Pimentel H, Kelley R, Salzberg SL. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 2013;14:R36." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d980e3770">56</a>] was used to align RNA-seq reads from tuber, flower (young), leaf (young), stem, leaf (old), and flower (old) samples to the <i>D. rotundata</i> reference genome, and Cufflinks 2.2.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Trapnell C, Roberts A, Goff L, Pertea G, Kim D, Kelley DR, et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat Protoc. 2012;7:562–78." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d980e3776">57</a>] was used to generate gene models from these data. The evidence-based predictions using the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments (PASA) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Haas BJ, Delcher AL, Mount SM, Wortman JR, Smith Jr RK, et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 2003;31:5654–66." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d980e3780">58</a>] were generated in a Trinity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Haas BJ, Papanicolaou A, Yassour M, Grabherr M, Blood PD, Bowden J, et al. De novo transcript sequence reconstruction from RNA-seq using the Trinity platform for reference generation and analysis. Nat Protoc. 2013;8:1494–512." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d980e3783">59</a>] assembled transcriptome from the RNA-seq data. JIGSAW 3.2.9 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Allen JE, Salzberg SL. JIGSAW: integration of multiple sources of evidence for gene prediction. Bioinformatics. 2005;21:3596–603." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d980e3786">60</a>] was used to select and combine the gene models obtained using the three approaches with the weighting values assigned to the results from FGENESH, AUGUSTUS, and PASA of 10, 3, and 3, respectively. In total, 21,882 consensus gene models were predicted. These gene models were further improved upon using the MAKER [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Holt C, Yandell M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics. 2011;12:491." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d980e3789">61</a>] pipeline (Additional file 2: Figure S14). Publicly available ESTs and protein sequences from related plant species were aligned to the genome using GMAP [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Wu TD, Watanabe CK. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics. 2005;21:1859–75." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d980e3792">62</a>] and Exonerate 2.2.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Slater GS, Birney E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics. 2005;6:31." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d980e3795">63</a>], respectively. De novo and reference-guided transcripts were assembled from RNA-seq data from all 18 tissues using Bowtie 1.1.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Langmead B, Trapnell C, Pop M, Salzberg SL. Ultrafast and memory-efficient alignment of short DNA sequences to the human genome. Genome Biol. 2009;10:R25." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d980e3799">64</a>], Trinity 2.0.6 and SAMtools 1.2.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li H. A statistical framework for SNP calling, mutation discovery, association mapping and population genetical parameter estimation from sequencing data. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:2987–93." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d980e3802">65</a>], and Trinity 2.0.6 and TopHat 2.1.0, respectively. Both sets of assembled transcripts were used to build a comprehensive transcript database using PASA (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S13). High-quality non-redundant transcripts from PASA were used to generate a training set for AUGUSTUS 3.1. Gene models were predicted twice using the genome, improved repeat sequences, assembled transcripts, EST and protein alignments, the AUGUSTUS training set, and a legacy set of 21,882 gene models obtained previously using MAKER 2.31.6 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Holt C, Yandell M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics. 2011;12:491." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d980e3808">61</a>], retaining all legacy gene models or querying them with new evidence and discarding those that could not be validated. From both MAKER runs, 21,894 and 76,449 gene models were predicted, respectively. A consensus set of gene models from both MAKER outputs was obtained using JIGSAW 3.2.9 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Allen JE, Salzberg SL. JIGSAW: integration of multiple sources of evidence for gene prediction. Bioinformatics. 2005;21:3596–603." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d980e3811">60</a>] at a 1:1 ratio. In total, 26,198 consensus gene models were predicted in the <i>D. rotundata</i> genome. The corresponding amino acid sequences were also predicted for these gene models. To confirm these gene models, the RNA-seq reads were aligned to the CDSs (coding sequences) of the predicted genes using BWA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Li H, Durbin R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics. 2010;26:589–95." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d980e3818">44</a>] with default parameters. Accordingly, 85.8% of the gene models could be aligned by at least a single RNA-seq read. Functional annotation of the amino acid sequences was performed using the in-house pipeline, AnnotF, which compares Blast2GO [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Conesa A, Götz S, García-Gómez JM, Terol J, Talón M, Robles M. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics. 2005;21:3674–76." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d980e3821">66</a>] and InterProScan [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Jones P, Binns D, Chang HY, Fraser M, Li W, McAnulla C, et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics. 2014;30:1236–40." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d980e3824">67</a>] functional terms.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Comparative genomics</h3><p>Pairwise orthology relationships were determined with Inparanoid [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Remm M, Storm CE, Sonnhammer EL. Automatic clustering of orthologs and in-paralogs from pairwise species comparisons. J Mol Biol. 2001;314:1041–52." href="#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d980e3836">68</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="O'Brien KP, Remm M, Sonnhammer EL. Inparanoid: a comprehensive database of eukaryotic orthologs. Nucleic Acids Res. 2005;33:D476–80." href="#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d980e3836_1">69</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Berglund AC, Sjölund E, Östlund G, Sonnhammer EL. InParanoid 6: eukaryotic ortholog clusters with in paralogs. Nucleic Acids Res. 2008;36:D263–66." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d980e3839">70</a>] using the longest protein-coding isoform for each gene in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (TAIR10) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Lamesch P, Berardini TZ, Li D, Swarbreck D, Wilks C, Sasidharan R, et al. The Arabidopsis Information Resource (TAIR): improved gene annotation and new tools. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012;40:D1202–10." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d980e3845">50</a>], <i>Oryza sativa japonica</i> (v7.0) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Ouyang S, Zhu W, Hamilton J, Lin H, Campbell M, Childs K, et al. The TIGR Rice Genome Annotation Resource: improvements and new features. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007;35:D883–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d980e3852">52</a>], <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> (v3.1) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Brachypodium distachyon v3.1 DOE-JGI, &#xA;                    https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html#!info?alias=Org_Bdistachyon/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 11 Sept 2017." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d980e3858">71</a>], <i>Musa acuminata</i> (v2) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Droc G, Larivière D, Guignon V, Yahiaoui N, This D, Garsmeur O, et al. The banana genome hub. Database. 2013;2013:bat035." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d980e3864">72</a>], <i>Elaeis guineensis</i> (EG5) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Singh R, Ong-Abdullah M, Low ET, Manaf MA, Rosli R, Nookiah R, et al. Oil palm genome sequence reveals divergence of interfertile species in Old and New worlds. Nature. 2013;500:335–39." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d980e3871">73</a>], and <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i> (DPV01) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Al-Mssallem IS, Hu S, Zhang X, Lin Q, Liu W, Tan J, et al. Genome sequence of the date palm Phoenix dactylifera L. Nat Commun. 2013;4:2274." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d980e3877">74</a>]. Orthology clusters across all seven species were determined using Multiparanoid [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Alexeyenko A, Tamas I, Liu G, Sonnhammer EL. Automatic clustering of orthologs and in paralogs shared by multiple proteomes. Bioinformatics. 2006;22:e9–15." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d980e3880">75</a>]. Sequences for the 12 classes of lectins were obtained from UniProt [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="The UniProt Consortium. UniProt: the universal protein knowledgebase. Nucleic Acids Res. 2017;45(D1):D158–69." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d980e3883">76</a>] for the proteomes of <i>A. thaliana</i> (up000006548), <i>B. distachyon</i> (up000008810), and <i>O. sativa</i> (up000059680). Protein alignments for B-lectin class protein sequences from all three of these species and <i>D. rotundata</i> were generated using the program Multiple Alignment using Fast Fourier Transform (MAFFT) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Katoh K, Standley DM. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol Biol Evol. 2013;30:772–80." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d980e3899">77</a>]. Maximum likelihood trees were constructed based on the concatenated alignments of all 378 B-lectin proteins using RAxML [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Stamatakis A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics. 2014;30:1312–3." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d980e3902">78</a>] 8.0.2 with 1000 bootstraps. Enrichment of tuber-specific genes was detected using TopHat 2.1.0 to align RNA-seq data from each of the 12 tissues to the genome, with one biological replicate for each tissue. HTSeq 0.6.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Anders S, Pyl PT, Huber W. HTSeq—a Python framework to work with high-throughput sequencing data. Bioinformatics. 2014;31:166–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d980e3905">79</a>] was used to generate raw counts. Then the Bioconductor package DESeq2 1.14.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Love MI, Huber W, Anders S. Moderated estimation of fold change and dispersion for RNA-seq data with DESeq2. Genome Biol. 2014;15:550." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d980e3909">80</a>] was used to compare raw counts of the three tuber tissues against all the other nine tissues (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S12) to determine tuber-enriched gene expression based on a log2 fold change &gt; 0 and Benjamini–Hochberg [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Benjamini Y, Hochberg Y. Controlling the false discovery rate: a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J R Stat Soc B Methodol. 1995;57(1):289–300." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d980e3915">25</a>] adjusted <i>P</i> value &lt; 0.05.</p><p>Gene enrichment analysis of orthology clusters was performed with GOATOOLS [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Tang H, Klopfenstein D, Pedersen B, Flick P, Sato K, Ramirez F, et al. GOATOOLS: Tools for Gene Ontology. Zenodo. 2015: &#xA;                    http://doi.org/10.5281/zenodo.31628&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d980e3924">81</a>], using the Holm significance test, and the false discovery rate was adjusted using the Benjamini–Hochberg procedure [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Benjamini Y, Hochberg Y. Controlling the false discovery rate: a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J R Stat Soc B Methodol. 1995;57(1):289–300." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d980e3927">25</a>]. The list of enriched genes was filtered for redundant Gene Ontology (GO) terms using REVIGO [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Supek F, Bošnjak M, Škunca N, Šmuc T. REVIGO summarizes and visualizes long lists of gene ontology terms. PLoS One. 2011;6:e21800." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d980e3930">82</a>]. For the species phylogeny, protein alignments for each gene with a 1:1 orthologous relationship across all monocot species were generated with MAFFT using the longest protein isoform. Maximum likelihood trees were constructed based on the concatenated alignments of 2381 orthologous protein-coding genes using RAxML 8.2.8 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Stamatakis A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics. 2014;30:1312–3." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d980e3933">78</a>] with a JTT + Γ model and 1000 bootstraps.</p><p>SynMAP [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Lyons E, et al. The value of nonmodel genomes and an example using SynMap within CoGe to dissect the hexaploidy that predates the rosids. Trop Plant Biol. 2008;1(3):181–90." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d980e3939">83</a>] using BLASTZ [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Schwartz S, Kent WJ, Smit A, et al. Human–mouse alignments with BLASTZ. Genome Res. 2003;13(1):103–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d980e3942">84</a>] alignments, DAGchainer [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Haas BJ, Delcher AL, Wortman JR, Salzberg SL. DAGchainer: a tool for mining segmental genome duplications and synteny. Bioinformatics. 2004;20(18):3643–6." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d980e3945">85</a>] (options -D 30 and -A 2), and no merging of syntenic blocks were used as part of the CoGe platform [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Lyons E, Freeling M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequences. Plant J. 2008;53(4):661–73." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d980e3948">86</a>] to identify syntenic blocks between the hard-masked pseudo-chromosomes of <i>D. rotundata</i> and scaffolds/contigs of <i>Oryza sativa japonica</i> (A123v1.0), <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> (v0.01), and <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i> L. (v3). A syntenic path assembly was then carried out on each of the same three species in SynMap using synteny between the scaffolds/contigs against <i>D. rotundata</i> pseudo-molecules. The syntenic path assembly is a reference-guided assembly that uses the synteny between two species to order and orientate contigs. This approach highlights regions of conservation that were otherwise too shuffled to be clearly observed. Self-self synteny analysis of <i>D. rotundata</i> pseudo-chromosomes was carried out using SynMap Last alignments with default parameters and syntenic gene pair synonymous rate change calculated by CodeML [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Yang Z. PAML 4: Phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol Biol Evol. 2007;24(8):1586–91." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d980e3970">87</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">RAD-based linkage mapping and scaffold anchoring</h3><p>RAD-seq was performed as previously described [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Matsumura H, Miyagi N, Taniai N, Fukushima M, Tarora K, Shudo A, et al. Mapping of the gynoecy in bitter gourd (Momordica charantia) using RAD-seq analysis. PLoS One. 2014;9:e87138." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d980e3981">88</a>] with a minor modification. Genomic DNA was digested with the restriction enzymes <i>Pac</i>I and <i>Nla</i>III to prepare libraries used to generate PE reads by Illumina HiSeq 2500 (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S6). Approximately 822.7-Mb and 250.4-Mb sequence reads covering 22.9% and 5.3% of the estimated 504-Mb <i>D. rotundata</i> genome sequence, excluding gap regions, at average depths of 7.2× and 9.8× were generated for the parental lines and F1 individuals, respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S7).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec22">Library preparation and sequencing</h4><p>For library construction, 1 μg DNA obtained from the two parental lines (TDr97/00917-P1 and TDr99/02627-P2) and the 150 F1 individuals was digested with <i>Pac</i>I, which recognizes 5’-TTAATTAA-3’, and a biotinylated adapter-1 was ligated to the digested DNA fragments. The adapter-1-ligated DNA fragments were digested with a second enzyme, <i>Nla</i>III (5’-CATG-3’). After collecting the biotinylated fragments using streptavidin-coated magnetic beads, adapter-2 was ligated to the <i>Nla</i>III-digested ends. The adapter-ligated DNA was amplified using primers containing sample-specific index sequences, adapter-1 (F) and adapter-2 (R) sequences, and sequences corresponding to the P7 and P5 primers for Illumina sequencing library preparation (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S6). The PCR products were pooled in equal proportions, purified, and subjected to PE sequencing on the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform. Detailed information about the primers (P7 and P5) used for Illumina library preparation are given in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S20.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec23">Identification of parental line-specific heterozygous markers</h4><p>RAD-tags were aligned to the <i>D. rotundata</i> reference genome using BWA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Li H, Durbin R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics. 2010;26:589–95." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d980e4033">44</a>]. The aligned data were converted to SAM/BAM files using SAMtools [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li H. A statistical framework for SNP calling, mutation discovery, association mapping and population genetical parameter estimation from sequencing data. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:2987–93." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d980e4036">65</a>], and the RAD-tags with mapping quality &lt; 60 or containing insertions/deletions in the alignment data were excluded from analysis. Low mapping positions including those with only a single RAD-tag and a mapping quality score of &lt; 30 were also excluded. SNP-index values [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Takagi H, Abe A, Yoshida K, Kosugi S, Natsume S, Mitsuoka C, et al. QTL-seq: rapid mapping of quantitative trait loci in rice by whole genome resequencing of DNA from two bulked populations. Plant J. 2013;74:174–83." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d980e4039">28</a>] were calculated at all SNP positions. For linkage mapping, two types of heterozygous markers (SNP-type and presence/absence-type) were identified (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S8). The SNP-type heterozygous markers were defined based on SNP-index patterns of the parental line RAD-tags. For example, positions with SNP-index values ranging from 0.2 to 0.8 in P1 but homozygous in P2 with SNP-index values of either 0 or 1 were defined as P1-specific heterozygous SNPs. A similar procedure was followed to identify P2-specific heterozygous SNP markers. The selected markers were filtered using depth information at all positions. To increase the accuracy of the selected markers, their segregation (1:1 ratio) was confirmed in 150 F1 individuals obtained from a cross between P1 and P2. If the segregation ratio was out of the confidence interval (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05) hypothesized by the binomial distribution, <i>B</i>(<i>n</i> = number of individuals, <i>P</i> = 0.5), the markers were excluded from further analysis. Only one marker was selected per 10-kb interval based on the number of F1 individuals represented and tag coverage. A total of 1105 and 990 P1- and P2-heterozygous SNP markers were selected, respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S7).</p><p>The presence/absence-type markers were defined based on the alignment depth of parental line RAD-tags. First, genomic positions that could be aligned by RAD-tags from only one of the parental lines were identified. Additionally, aligned tags should be heterozygous for that particular region. Similar to the SNP-type markers, the segregation patterns of candidate presence/absence-type markers in the F1 progeny were confirmed, and only those that segregated at a 1:1 ratio (as confirmed by binomial distribution filter) were retained. In the F1 progeny, positions with sequencing depths of ≥ 3 and 0 were defined as heterozygous and homozygous, respectively. For a given candidate position/marker, if the number of F1 individuals defined as homozygous or heterozygous was less than 120, the marker was excluded from further analysis. Only one heterozygous position was selected as a marker within a given 10-kb interval. In total, 221 and 282 positions were selected as P1- and P2-specific presence/absence-type heterozygous markers, respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S7).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec24">Linkage mapping</h4><p>To developing parental line-specific linkage maps, P1-Map and P2-Map, recombination fraction (<i>rf</i>) values between all pairs of markers on a given scaffold were calculated for both parents using the recombination pattern of the 150 F1 individuals. To minimize incorrect mapping, scaffolds were divided at positions where <i>rf</i> values exceeded 0.25 from the initial marker position (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S9). Only two flanking (distal) markers per scaffold were selected, corresponding to 477 and 493 P1- and P2-specific markers, respectively. These markers were used to develop P1 and P2 linkage maps according to the pseudo-testcross method [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Grattapaglia D, Sederoff R. Genetic linkage maps of Eucalyptus grandis and Eucalyptus urophylla using a pseudo-testcross: mapping strategy and RAPD markers. Genetics. 1994;137:1121–37." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d980e4084">18</a>] using the backcross model of R/qtl [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Broman KW, Wu H, Sen Ś, Churchill GA. R/qtl: QTL mapping in experimental crosses. Bioinformatics. 2003;19:889–90." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d980e4087">89</a>]. Due to the use of the pseudo-testcross method, the initial maps contained both the coupling and repulsion-type markers. Consequently, the genetic distance in linkage groups was larger than expected. To avoid the effect of repulsion-type markers when calculating genetic distances, these markers were converted to coupling-type markers. If a marker showed a high logarithm of odds (LOD) score and an <i>rf</i> value &gt; 0.5, it was defined as repulsion type and was therefore converted to the coupling-type genotype. This conversion was carried out gradually by changing the threshold of the LOD score from 10 to 5, and then to 3. After converting all repulsion markers to coupling markers, linkage maps were developed using markers showing LOD score &gt; 3 and <i>rf</i> value &lt; 0.25. Accordingly, a total of 21 and 23 linkage groups, each with a minimum of three markers, were generated for P1- and P2-Maps, respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S8 and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S10).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec25">Anchoring scaffolds</h4><p>To develop chromosome-scale pseudo-molecules, TDr96_F1 scaffold sequences were anchored onto the two parental-specific linkage maps using the selected RAD markers. To combine the two maps, the number of scaffolds shared between all possible linkage group (LG) pairs corresponding to the two maps was determined (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figures S11, S12). LG pairs that shared the largest number of scaffolds were combined using the same scaffolds. Each combined LG represented a pseudo-chromosome, which was designated/numbered according to the P1-Map LG designation (see Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig2">2</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S11). After combining the two maps to construct the pseudo-chromosomes, P1- and P2-specifc scaffolds were ordered according to their original order in their respective LGs. If the order of scaffolds could not be decided because the order was similar in both the P1- and P2-Maps, the order in P1-LG was adopted (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig2">2</a>). Finally, the ordered scaffolds were connected by 1000 nucleotides of “N” into a single fasta file for each pseudo-chromosome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S12).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">QTL-seq analysis</h3><p>DNA samples obtained from the two parental lines, TDr97/00917 (P3, female) and TDr97/00777 (P4, male), as well as samples pooled in equal amounts from 50 male (male-bulk) and 50 female (female-bulk) F1 individuals obtained from the cross between P3 and P4 were subjected to WGS. Libraries for sequencing were constructed from 1-μg DNA samples with a TruSeq DNA PCR-Free LT Sample Preparation Kit (Illumina) and were sequenced via 76 cycles on the Illumina NextSeq 500 platform. Short reads in which more than 20% of sequenced nucleotides exhibited a Phred quality score of &lt; 20 were excluded from further analysis. To perform QTL-seq analysis of F1 progeny, two types of analyses are required. In the first analysis, the SNP index and ΔSNP index are calculated at P4-specific heterozygous positions. The second analysis is performed using P3-specific heterozygous positions. To identify P4-specific heterozygous positions, the P3 “reference sequence” was first developed by aligning P3 reads to the reference genome sequence of <i>D. rotundata</i> and replacing nucleotides of the <i>D. rotundata</i> reference genome sequence with nucleotides of P3 at all SNP positions showing an SNP index of 1 (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S17c). SNP detection, calculation of SNP index, and replacement of SNPs were carried out via step 2 of QTL-seq pipeline version 1.4.4 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Iwate Biotechnology Research Center. &#xA;                    http://genome-e.ibrc.or.jp/home/bioinformatics-team/mutmap&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 1 Aug 2017." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d980e4145">90</a>]. Short reads obtained from both the male and female parents were then aligned to the “reference sequence” and heterozygous SNP positions between the two were extracted. A SNP was defined as heterozygous if the same position showed an SNP-index value ranging from 0.4 to 0.6 in one parent and a value of 0 in the second parent. Of the selected markers/positions, only those having enough depth in both parents (&gt;15) were used for analysis of SNP-index values in the bulk-sequenced samples. P3-specific heterozygous positions were identified similarly using the P4 “reference sequence.”</p><p>After identifying P4- and P3-specific heterozygous positions, the Illumina reads from the two bulk-sequenced samples (male and female bulks) were aligned to the reference sequences using BWA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Li H, Durbin R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics. 2010;26:589–95." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d980e4151">44</a>] and subjected to Coval filtering [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Kosugi S, Natsume S, Yoshida K, MacLean D, Cano L, Kamoun S, et al. Coval: improving alignment quality and variant calling accuracy for next-generation sequencing data. PLoS One. 2013;8:e75402." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d980e4154">91</a>] as previously described. When the P3 reference sequence was used for alignment, the SNP-index values were calculated only at all of the P4-specific heterozygous positions. By contrast, when the P4 reference sequence was used for alignment, the SNP-index values were calculated only at the P3-specific heterozygous positions. In both cases, positions with shallow depth (&lt; 6) in either of the two samples were excluded from analysis. The ∆SNP index was calculated by subtracting the SNP-index values of the male bulk from those of the female bulk. To generate confidence intervals of the SNP-index value, an in silico test simulating the application of QTL-seq to DNA bulked from 50 randomly selected F1 individuals was performed as described previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Takagi H, Abe A, Yoshida K, Kosugi S, Natsume S, Mitsuoka C, et al. QTL-seq: rapid mapping of quantitative trait loci in rice by whole genome resequencing of DNA from two bulked populations. Plant J. 2013;74:174–83." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d980e4157">28</a>] (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S22). The simulation test was repeated 10,000 times depending on the alignment depth of short reads to generate confidence intervals. These intervals were plotted for all SNP positions analyzed. Finally, sliding window analysis was applied to SNP-index, ∆SNP-index, and confidence interval plots with a 1-Mb window size and a 50-kb increment to generate SNP-index graphs (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S18).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Identification of putative W-region by de novo assembly of female and male parental genomes and mapping of bulked DNA from female and male F1 progeny</h3><p>DNA samples obtained from the two parental lines, TDr97/00917 (P3, female) and TDr97/00777 (P4, male), were separately subjected to de novo assembly. Libraries for sequencing were prepared with a TruSeq DNA PCR-Free LT Sample Preparation Kit (Illumina) and were sequenced for 251 cycles on the Illumina MiSeq platform. Contigs were generated using the DISCOVAR De Novo assembler [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Love RR, et al. Evaluation of DISCOVAR de novo using a mosquito sample for cost-effective short-read genome assembly. BMC Genomics. 2016;17(1):187." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d980e4174">29</a>], resulting in P3-DDN and P4-DDN, respectively. Separately, whole-genome resequencing of bulked DNA was performed on bulked DNA samples obtained from 50 female F1 (Female-bulk.fastq) and 50 male F1 (Male-bulk.fastq) progeny, all derived from a cross between P3 and P4. Two reference sequences, P3-DDN and P4-DDN, were combined to generate P3-DDN/P4-DDN. Short reads from the female and male bulks were separately mapped to P3-DDN/P4-DDN using the alignment software BWA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Li H, Durbin R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics. 2010;26:589–95." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d980e4177">44</a>]. After mapping, the MAPQ scores of the aligned reads were obtained. Under our conditions, if a short read was mapped to a unique position of the reference sequence, the MAPQ score was 60, whereas if the read was mapped to multiple positions, MAPQ was &lt; 60. Since two reference sequences (P3-DDN and P4-DDN) were fused to generate P3-DDN/P4-DDN, most genomic regions were represented twice. Therefore, most short reads mapped to two or more positions, leading to a MAPQ score &lt; 60. The reads that mapped to the P3-DDN/P4-DDN with MAPQ = 60 were judged to be located in either P3- or P4-specific genomic regions. After finding these P3- or P4-specific genomic regions, the depth of short reads that covered the regions for Female-bulk.fastq and Male-bulk.fastq, respectively, was evaluated. If the depth of Female-bulk.fastq was high and the depth of Male-bulk.fastq was 0 or close to 0, such genomic regions were retained as putative W-regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#Fig6">6</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S20).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">DNA markers linked to sex</h3><p>The primer sequences used for amplification of sex-linked markers sp1 and sp16, as well as the control <i>Actin</i> gene fragment (Dr-Actin), were as follows:</p><p>PCR primers for sp1 fragment:</p><ul class="u-list-style-none">
                    <li>
                      <p>sp1-F; 5’-GATCTGGCTTCCTCCATCTTG-3’</p>
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <p>sp1-R; 5’-GCTTGGGTGGTTAGTTTATTGTTTG-3’</p>
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <p>PCR primers for sp16 fragment:</p>
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <p>sp16-F; 5’-AATGTGTTTAACAGGGTGAATTC-3’</p>
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <p>sp16-R; 5’-GAATTCAGCCGAATATACTTATTC-3’</p>
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <p>PCR primers for Dr-Actin gene fragment:</p>
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <p>Dr-Actin-F; 5’-CAGGGAAAAGATGACCCAAATC-3’</p>
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <p>Dr-Actin-R; 5’-CCATCACCAGAATCCAGCAC-3’</p>
                    </li>
                  </ul>
                        <p>PCR was performed using the following conditions: 30 cycles of 98 °C for 10 s, 55 °C for 30 s, and 72 °C for 1 min. For CAPS analysis of the sp1 marker, the amplified DNA was digested with <i>Eco</i>RI. All PCR products were electrophoresed on 1.5% agarose gels.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Identification of SSR markers</h3><p>Approximately 4,932,582 bp simple sequence repeat (SSR) motif-containing sequences were predicted in the <i>D. rotundata</i> genome. Within this region, SSR sequences with enough flanking regions were identified and evaluated for use in primer design. Accordingly, 134,101 SSR-containing sequences, excluding those with single base repeats, were identified. The SSR information for these sequences was analyzed using GMATo [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Wang X, Lu P, Luo Z. GMATo: a novel tool for the identification and analysis of microsatellites in large genomes. Bioinformation. 2013;9:541–44." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d980e4269">92</a>] version 1.2 Build 20130106 with the following parameters: m (minimum motif length) = 2, x (maximum motif length) = 10, and r (minimum repeat number) = 10. The necessary information was obtained for 22,164 SSR-containing sequences in the assembled genome, 12,724 (57.4%) of which were anchored to the genetic map (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S19). Primer pairs were designed for 1000 of these sequences using Primer3 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Untergasser A, Cutcutache I, Koressaar T, Ye J, Faircloth BC, Remm M, et al. Primer3—new capabilities and interfaces. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012;40:e115." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-017-0419-x#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d980e4275">93</a>] software release 2.3.6 with the following parameters: product size = 100–500, primer length = 18–22 bp (optimum 20 bp), GC content = 40–60% (optimum 50%), and Tm = 57–63 (optimum = 60.0).</p></div></div></section>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;dopt=Abstract&amp;list_uids=24981608" aria-label="View reference 9 on PubMed" rel="nofollow">PubMed</a>

                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar1">Funding</h3>
                <p>This study (design of the study, collection, analysis, and interpretation of data, and manuscript writing) was funded by the Japan International Research Center for Agricultural Sciences (JIRCAS) and the International Institute of Tropical Agriculture (IITA) as a component of an international collaborative research project involving Iwate Biotechnology Research Center (IBRC), JIRCAS, and IITA entitled the “Use of genomic information and molecular tools for yam germplasm utilization and improvement for West Africa (EDITS-Yam).” Computations were partially performed on the National Institute of Genetics (NIG) supercomputer at ROIS (Research Organization of Information and Systems) National Institute of Genetics (Mishima, Shizuoka, Japan). Work performed at The Earlham Institute, Norwich, UK, was supported by strategic Biotechnology and Biological Sciences Research Council (BBSRC) funding (Institute Strategic Programme Grant BB/J004669/1 and iCASE Studentship BB/L017350/1) and by the Norwich Bioscience Institutes (NBI) Computing infrastructure for Science (CiS) group.</p>

class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Supplementary figures including a summary of world yam production and photos of yam markets in West Africa
